reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Triangular multiplicative update – outgoing edges.
    From AlphaFold2 Evoformer: updates pair (i,j) by aggregating over the
    shared index k via element-wise products of projected pair values along
    edges (i,k) and (j,k).
    """

    def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden_dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.layer_norm_in = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.proj_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.gate_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)
        self.layer_norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden_dim)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim)
        self.out_gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, pair_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_mask: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
            pair_mask: (B, N, N, 1)
        Returns:
            updated pair representation (B, N, N, pair_dim)
        """
        mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)
        x = self.layer_norm_in(pair_repr)

        a = self.proj_a(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_a(x)) * mask  # (B, N, N, H)
        b = self.proj_b(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_b(x)) * mask

        # Outgoing: aggregate over k with a[i,k] * b[j,k]
        out = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b)

        out = self.layer_norm_out(out)
        out = self.out_proj(out)
        out = torch.sigmoid(self.out_gate(pair_repr)) * out
        return out


pair_dim = 64
hidden_dim = 32
seq_len = 32
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    pair_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    pair_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len)
    return [pair_repr, pair_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [pair_dim, hidden_dim]

Phase 3: Apply output layer norm, out_proj, out_gate. - For each output element (i,j) that thread owns, we have O_val = O[i,j] (scalar for this h). We need to collect across h? Wait, out = out_proj(out) where out before projection has shape (B,N,N,H). out_proj is nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim). So output channel c = sum_h O[b,i,j,h] * W_out[h,c] + b_out[c]. This is a very nice per-thread computation! Because for each (b,i,j), we can accumulate in registers an array of H=32 floats. That's small. And we process each k sequentially. Let's outline the full warp-per-(b,i,j) kernel: BlockDim.x = H = 32 threads (one warp). GridDim = (B, N, N). Actually cuda grid is 3D: (N, N, B) or (B, N, N). We'll map accordingly. Kernel steps: For block (b, i, j): 1. threadIdx.x = h (0..31). 2. Initialize acc = 0.0f. 3. For k = 0 .. N-1: a. Load pair_repr[b, i, k, c] into smem_x_i. Compute LN for x_i: - Each thread has 2 elements: val0, val1. - Warp reduce sum of val0+val1 -> mean_i. Broadcast. - Warp reduce sum of (val0-mean)^2 + (val1-mean)^2 -> var_i. Broadcast. - Normalize: x0 = (val0 - mean) * rsqrt(var + eps) * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; similarly x1. - Write back to smem_x_i. Same for x_j. c. Compute mask values: mask_i = pair_mask[b,i,k] (unsqueezed). If pair_mask has 4 dims? We can handle both. Let's assume pair_mask is (B,N,N). mask_i = pair_mask[b,i,k]. Similarly mask_j = pair_mask[b,j,k]. d. Compute a_val for this h: dot_a = 0; dot_ga = 0; for c = 0..63: dot_a += smem_x_i[c] * W_a[c, h]; dot_ga += smem_x_i[c] * W_g_a[c, h]; a_val = (dot_a + b_a[h]) * sigmoid(dot_ga + b_g_a[h]) * mask_i; e. Compute b_val for this h: dot_b = 0; dot_gb = 0; for c = 0..63: dot_b += smem_x_j[c] * W_b[c, h]; dot_gb += smem_x_j[c] * W_g_b[c, h]; b_val = (dot_b + b_b[h]) * sigmoid(dot_gb + b_g_b[h]) * mask_j; f. acc += a_val * b_val; g. __syncthreads() before next k? Actually all threads read smem_x_i after writing it. Since all threads in block (warp) write and then read, warp-synchronous execution means no __syncthreads needed within a warp if no divergence. But to be robust, we can use __syncwarp(). 4. After k loop, we have acc per thread. 5. Apply out LN: - Mean of acc across warp: warpSum(acc)/H. - Var: warpSum((acc-mean)^2)/H. - acc = (acc - mean) * rsqrt(var + eps) * ln_out_w[h] + ln_out_b[h]. 6. So just linear on pair_repr. - We need to compute gate_c = sigmoid( sum_cin pair_repr[b,i,j,cin] * W_og[c, cin] + b_og[c] ). Let's compute this per c. - Since there are 64 channels and 32 threads, each thread can compute 2 channels. Let's assign thread h to compute c = h and c = h+32. - For each assigned c: dot = 0; for cin = 0..63: dot += smem_gate[cin] * W_og[c, cin]; gate_c = sigmoid(dot + b_og[c]); store gate_c in smem_gate[c] (reuse smem). - After all threads write, we have gate vector in smem_gate. 7. Compute out_proj and final output: - For each output channel c = 0..63: // compute sum_h acc_norm[h] * W_out[h, c] prod = acc * W_out[h, c]; sum_prod = warp_sum(prod); // warp shuffle reduce out_c = sum_prod + b_out[c]; final = out_c * smem_gate[c]; Write to output[b, i, j, c]. - This loop runs 64 iterations. Each iteration does a warp shuffle sum. That's okay. 8. End. This kernel has: - 2048 blocks (B*N*N). - Each block is 1 warp (32 threads). - Work per block: N (32) iterations of k, each iteration: - Load pair_repr for two positions (128 floats total). - 2 LNs over 64 elements. - 4 GEMV of size 64 (dot products) per thread: each thread does 4*64 = 256 FMAs. Over 32 k => 8192 FMAs per thread. - Warp reductions for LN. - After loop: warp reductions for out LN. - Compute gate: each thread does 2*64 = 128 FMAs. - Compute output: 64 iterations of warp reduction and 64 multiplies. Total FLOPs per block: ~10k. 2048 blocks => ~20M FLOPs. Very small. Memory reads: - pair_repr for all (b,i,k) and (b,j,k): 2048 blocks * 32 k * 2 positions * 64 floats = 8,388,608 floats = 32MB. - pair_repr for gate (b,i,j): 2048 * 64 = 131k floats = 0.5MB. - Weights: read repeatedly but cached. Total unique weight size ~ (64*32*4 + 64*64*2) ~ 16KB. - Writes: output 131k floats = 0.5MB. So memory traffic is dominated by pair_repr reads: 32MB. At 1.5TB/s, that's ~20 microseconds. PyTorch reference likely has much more traffic due to intermediate tensors and multiple kernels. So this kernel should be substantially faster. But will 2048 warps saturate GPU? 2048 warps = 2048 blocks * 1 warp. On A100, 108 SMs * 4 warps/SM = 432 warps can be concurrently scheduled (if occupancy 4). Actually A100 can have up to 64 warps per SM. With 1 warp per block, occupancy can be high if registers allow. So we can run many blocks in parallel. Total warps 2048, running on 108 SMs => ~19 warps per SM. That's plenty to hide latency if each warp has enough instructions. But note: each block is extremely small (1 warp). This is fine; modern GPUs handle it. Also, the kernel uses warp-level reductions. We need to implement warp shuffle functions. CUDA provides `__shfl_sync` (for compute capability >= 7.0). On Ampere/A100, it's supported. We have to be careful about `__syncthreads()`. Since the block is one warp, we may not need __syncthreads, but we can use `__syncwarp()` to be safe. However, using __syncthreads in a single warp is okay but has overhead. We can avoid it and rely on warp execution. Actually for the loop over k, we have: ``` for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { // load x_i and x_j into smem smem_x_i[threadIdx.x] = ...; smem_x_i[threadIdx.x + 32] = ...; // same for x_j // compute LN using warp shuffle // then dot product reading smem_x_i } ``` All threads in warp execute same instructions (no divergence). There is no write-after-read hazard across iterations because after loading new data into smem, all threads read it. Since the warp executes in lockstep, the writes happen before the reads in the same iteration. But the compiler might reorder? With __shared__ memory, within a warp, writes from one thread are visible to others without explicit sync because they are in the same warp. Actually CUDA guarantees that memory operations within a warp are visible to threads in the same warp without __syncthreads. So we can skip __syncthreads() entirely. However, when we store gate values in smem_gate, we need to make sure all threads have written before reading. Since each thread writes to different indices, and then we read, again within a warp it's okay. But there is a subtlety: if we use `smem_x_i` for both reading pair_repr and then writing normalized values back, there is a RAW hazard within the same thread. But it's fine because it's the same thread. So we can avoid syncs entirely, which is good. But we need to handle pair_mask shape. In get_inputs, pair_mask is (B,N,N). In forward, they do `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. So mask scalar for position (b,i,k) is `pair_mask[b,i,k]`. But if the test passes a mask of shape (B,N,N,1), `unsqueeze(-1)` would make (B,N,N,1,1). That's unlikely. We'll just handle both: if pair_mask.dim() == 3, access `pair_mask[b,i,k]`. If dim()==4, access `pair_mask[b,i,k,0]`. Actually it's safer to match reference exactly: `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)` if pair_mask.ndim == 3 else pair_mask`. Wait, if pair_mask is already (B,N,N,1), unsqueeze(-1) makes (B,N,N,1,1), which is wrong. But the docstring says (B,N,N,1). In get_inputs, it's (B,N,N). The reference code does `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. So if test gives (B,N,N,1), unsqueeze would be wrong. But likely test uses get_inputs which returns (B,N,N). So we can just do `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)` if pair_mask.dim() == 3 else pair_mask`. However within kernel we just need the scalar. Because the kernel is fused, we need to pass pair_mask as a tensor to the kernel. In host wrapper, we can check dimensions and maybe pass a pointer and a flag. Now about the output shape: (B, N, N, pair_dim). We'll allocate the output tensor in host wrapper and pass it. Let's think about elementwise operations: `sigmoid`. We can use `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))`. Precision: we need fp32 matching within 1e-4. LayerNorm uses `eps=1e-5` by default in PyTorch. Actually PyTorch LayerNorm default eps is 1e-5. We must use same. Also, PyTorch linear uses `x @ W^T + b`. Wait: `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)` computes `y = x A^T + b` where `A` is of shape `(out_features, in_features)`. So the weight tensor is `(out_features, in_features)`. In our kernel, when we compute `dot(x, W[:, h])`, we need to ensure we read the correct elements. Let's inspect PyTorch weight layout: - `self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim)` => weight shape (hidden_dim, pair_dim). In memory, it's contiguous with shape [hidden_dim, pair_dim]. So `W_a[h, c]` is memory location `h * pair_dim + c`. - In our kernel, each thread handles a fixed h and loops over c from 0 to C-1. It needs to read weight for that h and all c. So memory access pattern: for each c, read `W_a[h, c]`. Since h is fixed, this is a strided access with stride = pair_dim (64). Not contiguous across c. But since we loop over c, it's contiguous in memory? Actually row-major: adjacent c for same h are contiguous. Yes: `W_a[h, c]` is offset `h*C + c`. So for fixed h and varying c, it's contiguous. Good. So each thread reads a contiguous chunk of length C=64. That's great. Similarly for `gate_a` weight: shape (hidden_dim, pair_dim). Same. `proj_b`, `gate_b` same. `out_proj = nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim)` => weight shape (pair_dim, hidden_dim). So `W_out[c, h]` is offset `c*H + h`. In our output loop, for each c, thread h reads `W_out[c, h]`. Since h varies across threads, for a fixed c, the addresses are contiguous across the warp (c*H + 0, c*H + 1, ...). So coalesced. `out_gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)` => weight shape (pair_dim, pair_dim). For gate computation, each thread handles two c values. For each c, it loops over cin and reads `W_og[c, cin]`, offset `c*C + cin`. For fixed c, contiguous across cin. Good. LayerNorm weights: `ln_in` weight shape (pair_dim,). `ln_out` weight shape (hidden_dim,). Mask: The mask multiplication is `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. If pair_mask is (B,N,N), mask is (B,N,N,1). Then `a = ... * mask`. So we just multiply by `pair_mask[b,i,k]` (float). One more thing: The reference computes `layer_norm_in(pair_repr)` and then projections. Since x is used for both a and b. In our kernel, for each k, we compute LN on x_i and x_j separately. That's fine. Implementation details for warp shuffle reductions: ``` __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } ``` For mean and var over 64 elements with 32 threads, each thread holds 2 elements. Compute sum: ``` float sum = val0 + val1; sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); float mean = sum / 64.0f; ``` Compute var: ``` float sq0 = (val0 - mean) * (val0 - mean); float sq1 = (val1 - mean) * (val1 - mean); float var_sum = warp_reduce_sum(sq0 + sq1); float var = var_sum / 64.0f; ``` Then normalize each. For the output LN over H=32 elements, each thread holds 1 element (acc). So: ``` float sum = warp_reduce_sum(acc); float mean = sum / 32.0f; float diff = acc - mean; float sq = diff * diff; float var = warp_reduce_sum(sq) / 32.0f; acc = diff * rsqrtf(var + eps) * ln_out_w[h] + ln_out_b[h]; ``` Now, we need to write the host wrapper and CUDA kernel. Given the use of `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`, we need to provide C++ source that defines the host function. The auto-generated pybind will create a Python function with the given signature. We need to handle at::Tensor objects. Let's draft the CUDA code. First, define the kernel: ``` template <int N=32, int C=64, int H=32> __global__ void fused_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, int mask_ndim, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_b, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B) { // block indices int b = blockIdx.z; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.x; int h = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // compute strides int stride_N = N; int stride_C = C; int stride_repr = N * N * C; int stride_mask = N * N; // if 3d // smem extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C] float* smem_x_j = smem + C; // [C] float* smem_gate = smem + 2*C; // [C] // total 3*C floats float acc = 0.0f; const float eps = 1e-5f; for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { // load pair_repr for (b,i,k,*) and (b,j,k,*) int base_i = b*stride_repr + i*N*C + k*C; int base_j = b*stride_repr + j*N*C + k*C; smem_x_i[h] = pair_repr[base_i + h]; smem_x_i[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_i + h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = pair_repr[base_j + h]; smem_x_j[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_j + h + 32]; // layer norm for x_i float v0 = smem_x_i[h]; float v1 = smem_x_i[h + 32]; float sum = v0 + v1; // warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / (float)C; float d0 = v0 - mean; float d1 = v1 - mean; float sq = d0*d0 + d1*d1; // warp reduce sq #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } float var = sq / (float)C; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_i[h] = v0; smem_x_i[h + 32] = v1; // layer norm for x_j v0 = smem_x_j[h]; v1 = smem_x_j[h + 32]; sum = v0 + v1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } mean = sum / (float)C; d0 = v0 - mean; d1 = v1 - mean; sq = d0*d0 + d1*d1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } var = sq / (float)C; inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = v0; smem_x_j[h + 32] = v1; // mask float mask_i, mask_j; if (mask_ndim == 3) { mask_i = pair_mask[b*N*N + i*N + k]; mask_j = pair_mask[b*N*N + j*N + k]; } else { mask_i = pair_mask[b*N*N + i*N + k]; // if 4d but last dim 1, same indexing } // Actually if mask is (B,N,N,1), indexing is b*N*N*1 + i*N*1 + k*1 + 0 = b*N*N + i*N + k. So same. // We'll just compute offset = b*N*N + i*N + k. // compute a_val float dot_a = 0.0f; float dot_ga = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_i[c]; dot_a += xv * proj_a_w[h*C + c]; // Wait: proj_a_w shape (H, C). At (h,c) dot_ga += xv * gate_a_w[h*C + c]; } float a_val = (dot_a + proj_a_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_ga + gate_a_b[h])))) * mask_i; // compute b_val float dot_b = 0.0f; float dot_gb = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_j[c]; dot_b += xv * proj_b_w[h*C + c]; dot_gb += xv * gate_b_w[h*C + c]; } float b_val = (dot_b + proj_b_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_gb + gate_b_b[h])))) * mask_j; acc += a_val * b_val; } // output layer norm over H=32 float sum_out = acc; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_out += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_out, offset); } float mean_out = sum_out / (float)H; float diff_out = acc - mean_out; float sq_out = diff_out * diff_out; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_out += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_out, offset); } float var_out = sq_out / (float)H; acc = diff_out * rsqrtf(var_out + eps) * ln_out_w[h] + ln_out_b[h]; // load pair_repr[b,i,j,:] for out_gate int base_ij = b*stride_repr + i*N*C + j*C; float g0 = pair_repr[base_ij + h]; float g1 = pair_repr[base_ij + h + 32]; smem_gate[h] = g0; smem_gate[h + 32] = g1; // compute out_gate (linear on original pair_repr) // each thread computes 2 channels: c = h and c = h+32 // We'll store results back in smem_gate float dot_og0 = 0.0f; float dot_og1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_gate[c]; dot_og0 += xv * out_gate_w[h*C + c]; // c_out = h dot_og1 += xv * out_gate_w[(h+32)*C + c]; // c_out = h+32 } smem_gate[h] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og0 + out_gate_b[h]))); smem_gate[h + 32] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og1 + out_gate_b[h + 32]))); // compute out_proj and write output // For each c in 0..C-1: // sum_h acc[h] * out_proj_w[c*H + h] // out = sum + b_out[c] // out *= smem_gate[c] // output[b,i,j,c] = out // Each thread can compute one c? But we have 32 threads, so each thread handles 2 c's. // However we want coalesced writes: output channels are contiguous in memory. So assigning thread h to c=h and c=h+32 gives strided writes (thread 0 writes c=0 and c=32). That's okay but not coalesced for writing two elements per thread? Actually writing to c=0 and c=32 are 128 bytes apart. Not ideal but fine for small data. // Alternatively, we can have a warp-level loop where each iteration computes one c and all threads participate. // Let's do a loop over c=0..63, using warp shuffle to get sum_h. // But this loop has 64 iterations * warp shuffle (5 shuffles) = 320 shuffles, which is okay but adds some overhead. // Let's do per-thread 2 channels to reduce warp reductions. // Wait, but if we compute per thread 2 channels, we need to read out_proj_w for each c. That's messy because c0 depends on thread index. // Let's instead compute both channels using warp reduction: ``` Wait, per-thread 2 channels approach for out_proj is a bit tricky because to compute the output for channel c, we need the dot product of acc (size H) with W_out[c,:]. Since each thread holds one element of acc, the warp reduction naturally computes the full dot product for a given c. If we want each thread to compute two channels independently, each thread would need the full acc vector (or do two warp reductions). It can do two warp reductions: one for c0 and one for c1. We also need to compute `gate_c` before that. We already stored gate in `smem_gate`. This approach uses shared memory: `smem_acc[32]`. We already have `smem` of size 3*C = 192 floats. We can add `smem_acc` at the end. Total shared memory: 192 + 32 = 224 floats = 896 bytes. Still tiny. Wait, but we need to store `smem_acc` only after the k loop, and before the c loop. We can reuse some of the other smem arrays. For example, after k loop, `smem_x_i` and `smem_x_j` are no longer needed. So we can alias `smem_acc` to `smem_x_i` or `smem_x_j`. That's fine. Let's restructure shared memory: ``` extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_buf = smem; // size 2*C + C = 3*C // During k loop: smem_buf[0..C-1] = x_i, smem_buf[C..2*C-1] = x_j, smem_buf[2*C..3*C-1] = gate (or reuse later) // After k loop, reuse smem_buf[0..H-1] for acc. ``` Actually we need gate during the output loop, which comes after out LN. We can compute gate and store in `smem_buf[2*C ... 3*C-1]` (size C). After k loop, x_i and x_j are no longer needed, so `smem_buf[0..C-1]` and `smem_buf[C..2*C-1]` can be reused, but we need `smem_buf[2*C..3*C-1]` for gate. That's fine. Now, the output computation: ``` // store acc into smem_acc (alias to smem_buf) float* smem_acc = smem_buf; smem_acc[h] = acc; __syncwarp(); // not needed within warp, but safe for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float prod = smem_acc[h] * out_proj_w[c*H + h]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { prod += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, prod, offset); } if (h == 0) { float out_val = prod + out_proj_b[c]; out_val *= smem_gate[c]; // smem_gate is smem_buf + 2*C output[base_ij + c] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, we need `smem_gate[c]`. `smem_gate` occupies `smem_buf + 2*C` (size C). Since C=64, `smem_buf` total size is at least 3*C = 192 floats. But `smem_acc` is alias to `smem_buf[0..H-1]` (first 32 floats). That's within the x_i region. `smem_gate` is at offset `2*C` = 128. So no overlap. However, `__shfl_down_sync` reduces across warp, leaving sum in lane 0. We only need lane 0 to write. This avoids needing to broadcast. Good. Now, the gate computation: - Load pair_repr[b,i,j,c] into `smem_gate` (size C). Each thread loads 2 elements: `smem_gate[h] = pair_repr[base_ij + h]; smem_gate[h+32] = pair_repr[base_ij + h + 32];`. - Compute gate values in place: each thread computes two channels. For c = h: dot = sum_cin smem_gate[cin] * out_gate_w[h*C + cin]; smem_gate[h] = sigmoid(dot + out_gate_b[h]); For c = h+32: dot = sum_cin smem_gate[cin] * out_gate_w[(h+32)*C + cin]; smem_gate[h+32] = sigmoid(dot + out_gate_b[h+32]); This uses `out_gate_w` shaped (C, C). Row `c` is `c*C + cin`. Since each thread handles two rows, reads are strided across threads but rows are contiguous in memory. Since weight is small, okay. Now, the mask. The `pair_mask` can be 3D or 4D. Let's handle generically by computing mask offset. In reference `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`, if `pair_mask` is (B,N,N), then mask offset for (b,i,k) is `b*N*N + i*N + k`. If `pair_mask` is already (B,N,N,1), the offset is the same because the last dimension size is 1. So we can just index using `b*N*N + i*N + k`. But what if `pair_mask` is (B,N,N,1) stored as 4D? In PyTorch, it's contiguous with shape (B,N,N,1). The element at (b,i,k,0) is at offset `((b*N + i)*N + k)*1 + 0 = b*N*N + i*N + k`. So same offset. Great. So we don't need a special case. We just need to make sure we pass the correct pointer. We can even pass `pair_mask` as a 1D contiguous float tensor. However, what if `pair_mask` is bool? In get_inputs, it's `torch.ones(...)` which is float by default? Actually `torch.ones` returns float. But `pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)` gives float. The multiplication with a (float) works. If test passes bool mask, unsqueeze gives bool, then multiplication would cast. In our kernel we can treat mask as float. We can call `.float()` in host wrapper if needed. But to be safe, in the host wrapper, we can do: ``` if (pair_mask.scalar_type() != torch::kFloat) { pair_mask = pair_mask.to(torch::kFloat); } ``` This ensures contiguous float. Now, we need to handle the dimensions passed to the kernel. The grid is `(N, N, B)`. Actually cuda grid max dims: `gridDim.x`, `gridDim.y`, `gridDim.z`. We'll map: ``` dim3 grid(N, N, B); // blockIdx.x = j, blockIdx.y = i, blockIdx.z = b? Actually we used i=blockIdx.y, j=blockIdx.x. That's fine. dim3 block(H); // 32 threads ``` Wait, in my earlier plan, blockIdx.x = j, blockIdx.y = i, blockIdx.z = b. Yes. Now, strides for pair_repr: shape (B, N, N, C). It's contiguous. So offset = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * C + c. Similarly for pair_mask: shape (B, N, N). Offset = (b * N + i) * N + k. Output: same as pair_repr. Now, a potential issue: The kernel uses dynamic shared memory: `extern __shared__ float smem[];`. The size required is `3 * C * sizeof(float)` = 768 bytes. We will pass this in launch config. But note: `smem_acc` alias after k loop is okay because we don't use x_i and x_j after the loop. We don't need a __syncthreads because it's one warp. But to be safe, since we immediately overwrite smem_acc and then read it in the loop over c, there is no hazard. However, we need to make sure that when we write `smem_gate` after k loop, it's after we are done using x_i/x_j. The gate computation reads pair_repr into `smem_gate` (which is separate from x_i/x_j). So fine. Now, one question: the mask in reference is `pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. If `pair_mask` is shape (B,N,N), then `mask` shape is (B,N,N,1). In code, `a = ... * mask`. So mask broadcasts over last dimension (hidden). We multiply by `mask_i` scalar. Good. But wait: `pair_mask` in the reference is shape (B,N,N,1) according to docstring, but in get_inputs it's (B,N,N). In forward, unsqueeze(-1) adds a dimension at the end. So if input is (B,N,N,1), unsqueeze gives (B,N,N,1,1) which may not broadcast correctly with (B,N,N,H). Actually `mask.unsqueeze(-1)` on a (B,N,N,1) tensor yields (B,N,N,1,1). Multiplying with (B,N,N,H) may broadcast if the last dimension 1 is matched? Let's see: (B,N,N,1,1) vs (B,N,N,H). The shapes have different ranks: 5 vs 4. PyTorch broadcasting aligns from the right: dimensions: ... 1 vs H (mismatch? No, 1 can broadcast to H). Then 1 vs N? Actually aligning: Tensor1: B, N, N, 1, 1 Tensor2: B, N, N, H Wait, alignment from right: - dim -1: 1 vs H -> broadcast ok. - dim -2: 1 vs N -> broadcast ok? Actually tensor2 has only 4 dims: (B,N,N,H). So from right: -1: 1 vs H -> ok -2: 1 vs N -> ok -3: N vs N -> ok -4: B vs B -> ok So it does broadcast! Wait, does PyTorch allow broadcasting tensors of different ranks? Yes, by prepending 1s to the shorter shape. So (B,N,N,H) is treated as (B,N,N,H) and (B,N,N,1,1) is treated as (B,N,N,1,1). When aligning from the right, we compare H vs 1 (ok), N vs 1 (ok), N vs N, B vs B. So it broadcasts. But is this intended? The code likely expects pair_mask to be 3D, but even if 4D with trailing 1, unsqueeze(-1) makes it 5D with trailing 1,1. That still works. Anyway, our kernel just uses `pair_mask[b*N*N + i*N + k]` which corresponds to the first element of the trailing dimensions. Since trailing dims are size 1, that's the only element. Works for any shape as long as the first three dims are B,N,N. But if pair_mask is 5D, offset would be different. Let's assume standard. Let's refine the CUDA kernel code. ``` __global__ void fused_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_b, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B) { const int N = 32; const int C = 64; const int H = 32; int b = blockIdx.z; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.x; int h = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int stride_repr = N * N * C; int stride_mask = N * N; int base_ij = b * stride_repr + i * N * C + j * C; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C] float* smem_x_j = smem + C; // [C] float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * C; // [C] float acc = 0.0f; const float eps = 1e-5f; for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { int base_i = b * stride_repr + i * N * C + k * C; int base_j = b * stride_repr + j * N * C + k * C; // load pair_repr for i and j positions smem_x_i[h] = pair_repr[base_i + h]; smem_x_i[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_i + h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = pair_repr[base_j + h]; smem_x_j[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_j + h + 32]; // layer norm in for x_i float v0 = smem_x_i[h]; float v1 = smem_x_i[h + 32]; float sum = v0 + v1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } float mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C); float d0 = v0 - mean; float d1 = v1 - mean; float sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } float var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_i[h] = v0; smem_x_i[h + 32] = v1; // layer norm in for x_j v0 = smem_x_j[h]; v1 = smem_x_j[h + 32]; sum = v0 + v1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C); d0 = v0 - mean; d1 = v1 - mean; sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C); inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = v0; smem_x_j[h + 32] = v1; // mask float mask_i = pair_mask[b * stride_mask + i * N + k]; float mask_j = pair_mask[b * stride_mask + j * N + k]; // compute a_val for this h float dot_a = 0.0f; float dot_ga = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_i[c]; dot_a += xv * proj_a_w[h * C + c]; dot_ga += xv * gate_a_w[h * C + c]; } float a_val = (dot_a + proj_a_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_ga + gate_a_b[h])))) * mask_i; // compute b_val float dot_b = 0.0f; float dot_gb = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_j[c]; dot_b += xv * proj_b_w[h * C + c]; dot_gb += xv * gate_b_w[h * C + c]; } float b_val = (dot_b + proj_b_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_gb + gate_b_b[h])))) * mask_j; acc += a_val * b_val; } // output layer norm over H=32 float sum_out = acc; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_out += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_out, offset); } float mean_out = sum_out * (1.0f / (float)H); float diff_out = acc - mean_out; float sq_out = diff_out * diff_out; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_out += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_out, offset); } float var_out = sq_out * (1.0f / (float)H); acc = diff_out * rsqrtf(var_out + eps) * ln_out_w[h] + ln_out_b[h]; // load pair_repr[b,i,j,:] for out_gate smem_gate[h] = pair_repr[base_ij + h]; smem_gate[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_ij + h + 32]; // compute out_gate into smem_gate float dot_og0 = 0.0f; float dot_og1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_gate[c]; dot_og0 += xv * out_gate_w[h * C + c]; dot_og1 += xv * out_gate_w[(h + 32) * C + c]; } smem_gate[h] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og0 + out_gate_b[h]))); smem_gate[h + 32] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og1 + out_gate_b[h + 32]))); // store acc into smem (reuse smem_x_i) float* smem_acc = smem_x_i; smem_acc[h] = acc; // out_proj + final multiply and write for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float prod = smem_acc[h] * out_proj_w[c * H + h]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { prod += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, prod, offset); } if (h == 0) { float out_val = prod + out_proj_b[c]; out_val *= smem_gate[c]; output[base_ij + c] = out_val; } } } ``` Wait, there is a subtle issue. In the gate computation, `out_gate_w` shape is (C_out, C_in) = (64,64). We are reading `out_gate_w[h*C + c]` where h is 0..31. For `dot_og1`, we read `out_gate_w[(h+32)*C + c]`. That's fine. However, the `for (int c=0; c<C; ++c)` loops over `c`. Since threads in a warp all execute the same loop and read `smem_gate[c]` (shared memory broadcast), and each thread reads its own row of `out_gate_w`. The accesses to `out_gate_w` for a fixed c vary across threads: thread h reads `h*C + c`, thread h+1 reads `(h+1)*C + c`. Since c is the loop variable, the actual memory addresses are `h*C + c`. For a given c, these addresses are strided by C=64 floats (256 bytes). That's not coalesced. But again, weight is small. But wait: we could transpose the loop: for each thread, loop over c and read `out_gate_w[c*C + ???]`. But `out_gate_w` is (C_out, C_in), so row `c_out` is contiguous. In `nn.Linear`, weight is (out_features, in_features). So `out_gate_w[c_out * C_in + c_in]`. To compute dot product for row c_out, we need to sum over c_in. So per thread, for a fixed c_out (h or h+32), we loop over c_in (c). That's exactly what we do. The memory access pattern for a given c_in is strided by C_in across threads. Not coalesced, but since it's small and likely cached, okay. However, if we want to optimize, we could have threads cooperate to load transposed weights into shared memory, but given 64x64 weights and tiny compute, it's not worth it. Now, the host wrapper. We need to extract pointers. Use `at::Tensor` and ensure contiguous float. ``` torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b, torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b, torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b, torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b, torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b, torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b, torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b, torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b) { // ensure float and contiguous auto repr = pair_repr.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat); auto mask = pair_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat); // weights are assumed float and contiguous // ... int B = repr.size(0); int N = 32; int C = 64; int H = 32; auto output = torch::empty_like(repr); dim3 grid(N, N, B); dim3 block(H); size_t smem_size = 3 * C * sizeof(float); fused_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( repr.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_b.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B); return output; } ``` Wait, we need to handle pair_mask shape. If pair_mask is 4D (B,N,N,1), we want to flatten it. Using `.contiguous()` and `.to(torch::kFloat)` will make it contiguous. But its size might include the trailing 1. When we index `b * stride_mask + i * N + k` where `stride_mask = N * N`, for a 4D tensor (B,N,N,1), the element at (b,i,k,0) is at offset `((b*N + i)*N + k)*1 + 0 = b*N*N + i*N + k`. So as long as we use `b*stride_mask + i*N + k`, it works even if the tensor is 4D with trailing 1. But if the tensor is not contiguous in the last dimension? We call `.contiguous()`. Good. But what if pair_mask is (B,N,N,1) and we compute `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)` in the reference? That gives (B,N,N,1,1). In our wrapper, we receive the `pair_mask` tensor directly (the argument to forward). The reference does `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. So if test passes the same `pair_mask` as reference, we'll just use it. Since the kernel only needs the scalar mask, it's fine. However, there's a nuance: the reference multiplies `mask` (which is unsqueezed) with `a` and `b`. The unsqueeze adds a dimension of size 1. In PyTorch, broadcasting works both left and right? Actually broadcasting aligns from the rightmost dimension. So `(B,N,N,H) * (B,N,N,1)` works because 1 broadcasts to H. If `pair_mask` is passed as `(B,N,N,1)`, then unsqueeze gives `(B,N,N,1,1)`, which also broadcasts with `(B,N,N,H)` because the extra leading 1s are prepended. So scalar mask per (b,i,k) is the same. Thus our kernel is correct. Now, we need to write host wrapper and kernel using `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. Since we want to call a CUDA kernel, we need both C++ host code and CUDA device code. In `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`, we can provide `cpp_sources` (C++ code) and `cuda_sources` (CUDA code). The `functions` argument lists the host functions to expose. In `cuda_sources`, we define the `__global__` kernel. In `cpp_sources`, we define the host function `forward_cuda` and the pybind wrapper. The docs say: "Do not write your own `PYBIND11_MODULE(...)` block in `cpp_sources`. `load_inline` auto-generates one from the `functions=[...]` argument; including your own causes a duplicate-symbol redefinition error". So we just define the C++ function `forward_cuda` in `cpp_sources`. It will be automatically bound. Actually, the typical pattern is: ``` torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="custom_kernel", cpp_sources=''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward_cuda(...); ''', cuda_sources=''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor forward_cuda(...) { // launch kernel } ''', functions=["forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--use_fast_math", "-O3"], verbose=True, ) ``` Wait, but the forward function that calls the kernel needs to be compiled as host code. `cpp_sources` is compiled with C++ compiler, `cuda_sources` with NVCC. If we define `forward_cuda` in `cpp_sources`, it can call the kernel declared in `cuda_sources`? For separate compilation units, we need the kernel forward declaration. Usually in `load_inline`, both sources are combined into one library. We can put the kernel declaration in `cpp_sources`, or put everything in `cuda_sources`. Actually, often people put the host wrapper in `cuda_sources` as well, because it's compiled by nvcc. But `functions` list refers to functions declared in the overall compilation. We can put the kernel in `cuda_sources` and the host wrapper also in `cuda_sources`. Or put host wrapper in `cpp_sources` with `extern` declaration for the kernel. But the simplest is to put everything in `cuda_sources`? Wait, `cpp_sources` is processed by the C++ compiler (g++), while `cuda_sources` by nvcc. If host wrapper uses CUDA syntax like `<<< >>>`, it must be in a `.cu` file compiled by nvcc. So we should put the host wrapper in `cuda_sources`. The `cpp_sources` can be empty or contain declarations if needed. But we can also put a thin C++ wrapper in `cpp_sources` and the actual launcher in `cuda_sources`. Hmm. Actually, `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` has both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. The `cuda_sources` are compiled by nvcc into a `.cu.o`, and `cpp_sources` by the C++ compiler. The final linking step links them together. So we can declare the kernel in `cpp_sources` as `void kernel(...)`, and define it and the host wrapper in `cuda_sources`. Or define everything in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty (or minimal includes). But we must list the host function in `functions`. It can be defined in `cuda_sources`. That's allowed; the function just needs to be extern "C" or C++ with pybind. `load_inline` will generate the pybind code to call it from Python. Let's check standard usage: typically `cpp_sources` contains the host function definitions that USE ATen but not CUDA device code. `cuda_sources` contains device kernels and wrappers that launch them. But you can also put the wrapper in `cpp_sources` as long as it calls `AT_DISPATCH` and uses `cudaLaunchKernel`. However, using `<<< >>>` requires nvcc. So easiest: put host wrapper in `cuda_sources` as well. Example from PyTorch docs: ``` cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void ... { } torch::Tensor my_op(torch::Tensor x) { ... } """ cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor my_op(torch::Tensor x); """ load_inline(name="my_op", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["my_op"]) ``` But here `cpp_sources` only has declaration. We can even omit the declaration in `cpp_sources` if load_inline handles it? The `functions` argument just gives names; the binding code needs to find the symbol. The binding code is generated and includes a declaration? Actually pybind auto-generates wrapper code that calls `forward_cuda(...)`. If the function is defined in `cuda_sources`, the linker will find it. We might need to declare it in `cpp_sources` for the C++ compiler to know about it before linking. But if the auto-generated binding code includes an implicit declaration or includes a header? Let's look at how load_inline works. `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` builds a temporary module. It writes `cpp_sources` to a `.cpp` file and `cuda_sources` to a `.cu` file. The `.cpp` file will include generated pybind code that calls the functions listed. It may need to see declarations. Usually, you put the declarations in `cpp_sources` (or `cuda_sources` if you want). The `.cpp` file may not include the `.cu` file. So to be safe, we put declarations in `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, ...); ``` And in `cuda_sources`, we define the kernel and host wrapper. Now, for compilation flags: we can pass `extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math']`. `--use_fast_math` may speed up `expf` and `rsqrtf`. It could cause slight numerical differences. The tolerance is 1e-4 for fp32. `--use_fast_math` is generally okay but may introduce >1e-4 error sometimes? Usually it's fine for rsqrt and exp. Let's enable it for speed. We can also pass `'-arch=sm_80'` but it might be auto-detected. Given we need to compile and test, we should write the full Python module. Let's write the Python code. ``` import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b, torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b, torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b, torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b, torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b, torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b, torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b, torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b ); """ cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> constexpr int N = 32; constexpr int C = 64; constexpr int H = 32; constexpr float EPS = 1e-5f; __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void fused_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_in_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_a_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_a_b, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_w, const float* __restrict__ proj_b_b, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_w, const float* __restrict__ gate_b_b, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_out_b, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_w, const float* __restrict__ out_proj_b, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_w, const float* __restrict__ out_gate_b, float* __restrict__ output, int B) { int b = blockIdx.z; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.x; int h = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int stride_repr = N * N * C; int stride_mask = N * N; int base_ij = b * stride_repr + i * N * C + j * C; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C] float* smem_x_j = smem + C; // [C] float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * C; // [C] float acc = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) { int base_i = b * stride_repr + i * N * C + k * C; int base_j = b * stride_repr + j * N * C + k * C; smem_x_i[h] = pair_repr[base_i + h]; smem_x_i[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_i + h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = pair_repr[base_j + h]; smem_x_j[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_j + h + 32]; // LN for x_i float v0 = smem_x_i[h]; float v1 = smem_x_i[h + 32]; float sum = v0 + v1; sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); float mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C); float d0 = v0 - mean; float d1 = v1 - mean; float sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1; sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq); float var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + EPS); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_i[h] = v0; smem_x_i[h + 32] = v1; // LN for x_j v0 = smem_x_j[h]; v1 = smem_x_j[h + 32]; sum = v0 + v1; sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C); d0 = v0 - mean; d1 = v1 - mean; sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1; sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq); var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C); inv_std = rsqrtf(var + EPS); v0 = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h]; v1 = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32]; smem_x_j[h] = v0; smem_x_j[h + 32] = v1; float mask_i = pair_mask[b * stride_mask + i * N + k]; float mask_j = pair_mask[b * stride_mask + j * N + k]; // a_val float dot_a = 0.0f; float dot_ga = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_i[c]; dot_a += xv * proj_a_w[h * C + c]; dot_ga += xv * gate_a_w[h * C + c]; } float a_val = (dot_a + proj_a_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_ga + gate_a_b[h])))) * mask_i; // b_val float dot_b = 0.0f; float dot_gb = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_j[c]; dot_b += xv * proj_b_w[h * C + c]; dot_gb += xv * gate_b_w[h * C + c]; } float b_val = (dot_b + proj_b_b[h]) * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_gb + gate_b_b[h])))) * mask_j; acc += a_val * b_val; } // output LN float sum_out = warp_reduce_sum(acc); float mean_out = sum_out * (1.0f / (float)H); float diff_out = acc - mean_out; float sq_out = diff_out * diff_out; sq_out = warp_reduce_sum(sq_out); float var_out = sq_out * (1.0f / (float)H); acc = diff_out * rsqrtf(var_out + EPS) * ln_out_w[h] + ln_out_b[h]; // out_gate smem_gate[h] = pair_repr[base_ij + h]; smem_gate[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_ij + h + 32]; float dot_og0 = 0.0f; float dot_og1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_gate[c]; dot_og0 += xv * out_gate_w[h * C + c]; dot_og1 += xv * out_gate_w[(h + 32) * C + c]; } smem_gate[h] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og0 + out_gate_b[h]))); smem_gate[h + 32] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(dot_og1 + out_gate_b[h + 32]))); // out_proj float* smem_acc = smem_x_i; // reuse smem_acc[h] = acc; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float prod = smem_acc[h] * out_proj_w[c * H + h]; prod = warp_reduce_sum(prod); if (h == 0) { float out_val = prod + out_proj_b[c]; out_val *= smem_gate[c]; output[base_ij + c] = out_val; } } } torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor pair_mask, torch::Tensor ln_in_w, torch::Tensor ln_in_b, torch::Tensor proj_a_w, torch::Tensor proj_a_b, torch::Tensor gate_a_w, torch::Tensor gate_a_b, torch::Tensor proj_b_w, torch::Tensor proj_b_b, torch::Tensor gate_b_w, torch::Tensor gate_b_b, torch::Tensor ln_out_w, torch::Tensor ln_out_b, torch::Tensor out_proj_w, torch::Tensor out_proj_b, torch::Tensor out_gate_w, torch::Tensor out_gate_b) { auto repr = pair_repr.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); auto mask = pair_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); int B = repr.size(0); auto output = torch::empty_like(repr); dim3 grid(N, N, B); dim3 block(H); size_t smem_size = 3 * C * sizeof(float); fused_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( repr.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_in_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_a_b.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), proj_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_w.data_ptr<float>(), gate_b_b.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_out_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_proj_b.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_w.data_ptr<float>(), out_gate_b.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B ); return output; } """ ``` Then compile: ``` custom_op = load_inline( name="fused_tri_mul_outgoing", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) ``` Then ModelNew: ``` class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden_dim): super().__init__() self.layer_norm_in = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim) self.proj_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim) self.gate_a = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim) self.proj_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim) self.gate_b = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden_dim) self.layer_norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden_dim) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(hidden_dim, pair_dim) self.out_gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim) def forward(self, pair_repr, pair_mask): # get weights and biases as contiguous float tensors ln_in_w = self.layer_norm_in.weight.contiguous().float() ln_in_b = self.layer_norm_in.bias.contiguous().float() proj_a_w = self.proj_a.weight.contiguous().float() proj_a_b = self.proj_a.bias.contiguous().float() gate_a_w = self.gate_a.weight.contiguous().float() gate_a_b = self.gate_a.bias.contiguous().float() proj_b_w = self.proj_b.weight.contiguous().float() proj_b_b = self.proj_b.bias.contiguous().float() gate_b_w = self.gate_b.weight.contiguous().float() gate_b_b = self.gate_b.bias.contiguous().float() ln_out_w = self.layer_norm_out.weight.contiguous().float() ln_out_b = self.layer_norm_out.bias.contiguous().float() out_proj_w = self.out_proj.weight.contiguous().float() out_proj_b = self.out_proj.bias.contiguous().float() out_gate_w = self.out_gate.weight.contiguous().float() out_gate_b = self.out_gate.bias.contiguous().float() return custom_op.forward_cuda(pair_repr, pair_mask, ln_in_w, ln_in_b, proj_a_w, proj_a_b, gate_a_w, gate_a_b, proj_b_w, proj_b_b, gate_b_w, gate_b_b, ln_out_w, ln_out_b, out_proj_w, out_proj_b, out_gate_w, out_gate_b) ``` Wait, need to make sure that when `pair_repr` is on CUDA, the tensors passed to the kernel are on CUDA. `.contiguous().float()` will allocate new tensors on the same device. Now, we need to ensure that the kernel compiles. Let's examine the code for issues. Issue 1: `constexpr int N = 32;` etc inside cuda source. That's fine. Issue 2: `__shfl_down_sync` requires compute capability >= 3.0, but we assume modern GPU. Good. Issue 3: The kernel uses dynamic shared memory `extern __shared__ float smem[];`. After the loop, lane 0 has the sum. The function returns `val`, which in lane 0 is the sum, but in other lanes it's partial. In our code, after `sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum);`, we do `float mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C);`. Since all lanes need the correct mean, we need to broadcast the sum from lane 0 to all lanes. Wait! Does `__shfl_down_sync` leave the sum only in lane 0? Yes. It does not broadcast to all threads. The other threads have the value after adding their neighbor at offset 1, which is not the total sum. Let's verify: - lane 0: val0 += val1 (offset 1) => sum of lane0+lane1. Then val0 += val2 (offset 2) => sum of lane0..lane3. Then offset 4, 8, 16. Finally lane0 has total sum. - lane 1: val1 += val2 (offset 1). Then val1 += val3 (offset 2). etc. At end, lane1 has sum of lanes 1,2,3,5,6,7...? Wait, not total. So after `warp_reduce_sum`, only lane 0 has the total sum. We need to broadcast it. In many code patterns, they do: ``` #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); ``` Now `val` in lane 0 has total sum. To get it in all lanes, we can do: ``` val = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, val, 0); ``` So we should modify `warp_reduce_sum` to broadcast, or add a broadcast step after each reduction. Alternatively, we can use `__shfl_xor_sync` which reduces and leaves the result in all lanes? Actually `__shfl_xor_sync` with offset 1,2,4,... will broadcast the total sum to all lanes if you do it correctly? Let's recall: if you do `val += __shfl_xor_sync(mask, val, offset)`, each thread adds the value from the thread `threadIdx.x ^ offset`. After full reduction, all threads have the total sum. Yes! Because XOR-based reduction is a butterfly pattern. So we can implement warp reduce with XOR, which automatically broadcasts the sum to all lanes. Let's change `warp_reduce_sum` to use `__shfl_xor_sync`: ``` __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } ``` After this, every lane has the total sum. This is cleaner and eliminates the need for broadcast. However, it uses `__shfl_xor_sync` which is available on sm_30+. Let's use this. Let's verify correctness: - offset 16: lane 0 gets val0+val16, lane 1 gets val1+val17, ..., lane 15 gets val15+val31. - offset 8: lane 0 gets (val0+val16)+(val8+val24), etc. - After all offsets, each lane has sum of all 32 lanes. Yes. So we should replace all `__shfl_down_sync` with `__shfl_xor_sync` in warp_reduce_sum, and then drop the separate broadcast. Wait, an important bug: in my kernel, I use `grid(N, N, B)` which is `dim3 grid(32, 32, B)`. `blockIdx.x` = j (0..31), `blockIdx.y` = i (0..31), `blockIdx.z` = b. In CUDA, `dim3` constructor is `(x, y, z)`. So `dim3 grid(N, N, B)` means `grid.x = N`, `grid.y = N`, `grid.z = B`. In the kernel: ``` int b = blockIdx.z; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.x; ``` This is correct. Another issue: `warp_reduce_sum` should probably be called with `float` and ensure all threads in warp participate. That is fine because block size is 32, one warp. One more issue: when I load `pair_repr` for `x_i` and `x_j`, I load `h` and `h+32`. But `threadIdx.x` runs from 0 to 31. So the whole C=64 vector is loaded cooperatively by the warp. This is fine, but I need to make sure `__syncthreads()` is called before reading other threads' values. But since I only access `smem_x_i[c]` where `c` is any lane's written index, I do need a `__syncthreads()` after writing all 64 elements. However, the warp memory operations are not guaranteed to be visible to other threads without a memory fence or barrier. Actually, within a warp, it's mostly okay if threads read after they write, but to be safe with shared memory, `__syncthreads()` is recommended. Since all threads in the block are in the same warp (32 threads), `__syncthreads()` is not strictly necessary if there are no divergent branches. But it's safer to include it. Actually, in my kernel, I never explicitly call `__syncthreads()`. After writing `smem_x_i[h]` and `smem_x_i[h+32]`, some threads might read `smem_x_i[c]` in the dot product loops before the writes are committed. Since they are all in the same warp and execute in lockstep (SIMD), the writes from previous instructions should be visible by the time the loop starts, because instruction ordering is preserved within a warp, and shared memory has some latency but usually visible to issuing warp. Still, to be safe and clear, I should add a `__syncthreads()` after loading. But adding `__syncthreads()` inside a loop can be expensive. Maybe OK for small N. Now, also note: `pair_mask` is `pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)` in reference. In the reference code: ``` mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1) ... a = self.proj_a(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_a(x)) * mask ``` So the mask shape is (B, N, N, 1). In my kernel, I multiply `a_val` by `mask_i` where `mask_i = pair_mask[b, i, k]`. This is equivalent because the mask is broadcast over h. Now, there is another subtle bug: `mask` in reference also has `mask_j` for `b`. But what about `pair_mask` shape? In `get_inputs`, `pair_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len)`. So shape is (B, N, N). Good. Wait, in the reference, `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1)`. Then `a = ... * mask`. If `pair_mask` contains zeros, `a` is zeroed. In my kernel, `mask_i` and `mask_j` are just floats. If `mask_i` is 0, `a_val` is 0. If `mask_j` is 0, `b_val` is 0. So the product is 0. Correct. But the reference has `a` masked by `mask_i` and `b` masked by `mask_j`, then product. So my implementation is correct. Now, the `with_cuda` issue: the `ImportError` about `CXXABI_1.3.15` not found. Looking at similar issues, adding `-static-libstdc++` to linker flags might help. Also, maybe I should use `extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math", "-Xcompiler", "-static-libstdc++"]`? No, the compiler options for CUDA host code are passed via `-Xcompiler`. But for linking, `extra_ldflags` is better. Let's try `extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++']`. Also, I should ensure `with_cuda=True` is passed to `load_inline`. But it defaults to `True` if `cuda_sources` is provided. Let me rewrite the kernel code with the linker flag.

Currently I use: ``` float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C] float* smem_x_j = smem + C; // [C] float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * C; // [C] ``` Since threads h=0..31 write to smem_x_i[h] and smem_x_i[h+32], there might be bank conflicts since h and h+32 map to the same bank (32 banks, so index n and n+32 are in the same bank). This means threads h and h+32 write to the same bank simultaneously if they execute in the same instruction group. But since threads 0..31 all write at once, and h+32 for h=0..31 gives 32..63, banks 32..63 mod 32 = banks 0..31. So thread h writes to bank h, thread h+32 writes to bank (h+32) mod 32 = bank h. Both threads write to the same bank! This is bad - it creates 2-way bank conflicts. To fix this, I can add a 32-float padding. For example: ``` float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C] at offset 0 float* smem_x_j = smem + C + 1; // [C] at offset 65 float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * (C + 1); // [C] at offset 130 ``` But actually, a better solution is to use float2 for the loads: ``` float2* pair_repr2 = (float2*)(pair_repr + base_i); float2 v2 = pair_repr2[h/2]; ``` Wait, with 32 threads, h=0..31. If each thread loads a float2, then we'd get 64 floats in one instruction, but threads are reading float2's at offsets 0, 2, 4, ... 30. These are all contiguous float2 (8 bytes each), so 16 bytes apart. That's coalesced. Actually, threads in a warp reading consecutive float2 elements: thread h reads at offset h * 2 floats. With 32 threads, that's 64 floats = 256 bytes. On A100, a single warp can read up to 128 bytes per transaction, so this might split into 2 transactions, but it's still coalesced. For shared memory, if I store as float2 array, then thread h writes to `smem_x_i2[h]` where smem_x_i2 is `float2*`. This would put 2 floats per bank, but consecutive threads write to consecutive float2 elements, which avoids bank conflicts. Alternatively, just change the layout so that thread h stores to indices h and h+32 with a stride that avoids conflicts. Like: ``` smem_x_i[h] = ...; smem_x_i[h + 32] = ...; // bank conflict since h+32 maps to same bank as h in 32-bank SMEM ``` A common fix is to pad by 1: ``` smem_x_i[h] = ...; smem_x_i[h + 33] = ...; // h+33 maps to bank (h+1) mod 32, no conflict ``` So instead of allocating [64], I allocate [64 + 2] and use [h] and [h + 34] or something. I think for now, the transposed weights optimization is the most impactful and simplest change. Let me implement it. Here's the plan for the updated kernel: Python changes: ```python proj_a_w = self.proj_a.weight.t().contiguous().float() gate_a_w = self.gate_a.weight.t().contiguous().float() proj_b_w = self.proj_b.weight.t().contiguous().float() gate_b_w = self.gate_b.weight.t().contiguous().float() out_gate_w = self.out_gate.weight.t().contiguous().float() ``` CUDA changes: 1. Rename the shared memory pointers with proper padding 2. Update weight access patterns: - `proj_a_w[h * C + c]` → `proj_a_w[c * H + h]` - Same for gate_a_w, proj_b_w, gate_b_w - `out_gate_w[h * C + c]` and `out_gate_w[(h+32) * C + c]` → `out_gate_w[c * C + h]` and `out_gate_w[c * C + (h+32)]` Wait, if I'm passing the transposed out_gate weight, its shape is (64, 64) and memory layout is row-major. The transposed weight `Wt = W.t().contiguous()` means `Wt[c, h] = W[h, c]`. So `Wt[c*C + h]` gives `W[h, c]`, which is correct. In CUDA: ```cpp // For proj_a float dot_a = 0.0f, dot_ga = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_x_i[c]; dot_a += xv * proj_a_w[c * H + h]; // transposed access dot_ga += xv * gate_a_w[c * H + h]; } ``` For out_gate: ```cpp float gate0 = 0.0f, gate1 = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float xv = smem_gate[c]; gate0 += xv * out_gate_w[c * C + h]; gate1 += xv * out_gate_w[c * C + h + 32]; } smem_gate[h] = sigmoid(gate0 + out_gate_b[h]); smem_gate[h + PAD + 32] = sigmoid(gate1 + out_gate_b[h + 32]); ``` Wait, I need to be careful with shared memory layout to avoid bank conflicts for the second write. For shared memory, I'll use a structure like: ```cpp struct SharedMem { float x_i[C + 1]; // 65 floats, padding to avoid bank conflict float x_j[C + 1]; // 65 floats float gate[C + 1]; // 65 floats float acc[H + 1]; // 33 floats }; ``` Actually, dynamic shared memory is just a float array. Let me do: ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_x_i = smem; // indices 0..63, but second half at 33..96 with padding ``` No, that's messy. Let me just use fixed offsets: ```cpp constexpr int SMEM_C = C + 1; // 65 float* smem_x_i = smem; // 0..64 float* smem_x_j = smem + SMEM_C; // 65..129 float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * SMEM_C; // 130..194 float* smem_acc = smem + 3 * SMEM_C; // 195..227 (only need 32) ``` But this still has a problem. `smem_x_i[h]` and `smem_x_i[h + 32]` map to banks `h` and `(h + 32) % 32 = h`, so same bank. Even with the larger allocation, `smem_x_i[32]` is at byte offset `32 * 4 = 128` from the start. Bank = (offset / 4) % 32 = 0. So `smem_x_i[0]` and `smem_x_i[32]` are both in bank 0. Conflict. To avoid this, I need a stride that changes the bank. If I store the second half starting at an offset of 1 float: ``` smem_x_i[h] = ...; // bank h smem_x_i[h + 33] = ...; // bank (h + 33) % 32 = (h + 1) % 32, no conflict ``` Then the array size needs to be at least 33 + 32 = 65 floats. And to access the second half, I'd use index h + 33. Wait, but then when I access `smem_x_i[c]` in the dot product loop (c from 0 to 63), c=0 accesses bank 0, c=32 accesses bank 0 again. But these are in different loop iterations, not simultaneously. Bank conflicts only matter when multiple threads in a warp access the same bank simultaneously. In the dot product loop, all threads read the SAME `smem_x_i[c]` simultaneously (broadcast). Broadcasts don't cause bank conflicts! The shared memory hardware handles broadcasts by sending the data to all requesting threads. So the bank conflict issue is ONLY for the writes: ``` smem_x_i[h] = pair_repr[base_i + h]; smem_x_i[h + 32] = pair_repr[base_i + h + 32]; ``` Here, threads 0..31 write to banks 0..31 for `smem_x_i[h]`, which is fine. Then all threads write to banks again for `smem_x_i[h + 32]`. Thread 0 writes to bank 0 (offset 32), thread 1 writes to bank 1 (offset 33), but thread 0 and thread 1 are writing simultaneously in lockstep. Wait, thread h writes to bank `(h + 32) % 32 = h % 32`. So all 32 threads write to banks 0..31, one thread per bank. Actually, this is NOT a bank conflict! Bank conflict occurs when multiple threads in a warp access DIFFERENT addresses in the SAME bank. Here, thread h accesses bank h for both the first and second write. But the two writes are sequential (warp32, then warp33). At any given cycle, thread h is accessing bank h. No two threads access the same bank in the same cycle for a single write instruction. Wait, but there are two write instructions. For the first write, thread h accesses bank h, offset h. That's 32 threads accessing 32 different banks - no conflict. For the second write, thread h accesses bank h, offset h+32. Again, 32 threads accessing 32 different banks - no conflict! Oh! I was wrong. The bank conflict only happens if two threads in the same instruction access the same bank. Here, in each instruction, each thread accesses a different bank. The fact that the first instruction also used those banks is irrelevant because they're different instructions. So there are NO bank conflicts in the original kernel! The only time there might be a conflict is if the bank calculation wraps differently. Shared memory bank = (byte_address / 4) % 32. For index h: bank = h % 32. For index h+32: bank = (h+32) % 32 = h % 32. So both index h and index h+32 map to bank h. But in the first write, thread h writes index h (bank h). In the second write, thread h writes index h+32 (bank h). Since these are sequential instructions, there's no conflict. However, if I had: ``` smem_x_i[h] = ...; smem_x_i[h + 32] = ...; ``` What if thread h reads `smem_x_i[h + 32]` while thread h+1 reads `smem_x_i[h + 33]` - still each thread reads a different bank. What about when all threads read `smem_x_i[c]` in the dot product loop? Threads 0..31 all read index c. If c < 32, all threads read bank c simultaneously. This is a broadcast, no conflict. If c >= 32, all threads read bank (c % 32) simultaneously. Also a broadcast, no conflict. So actually, there are NO bank conflicts in this kernel! Great. I don't need to worry about padding. One more thing: the shared memory size is 3 * 64 + 32 = 224 floats = 896 bytes. With 32 threads per block, that's less than 1KB, trivial. OK let me write the final kernel with transposed weights. I'll also make sure to use `smem_gate[h + 32]` correctly without padding concerns, since there are no bank conflicts. Also, for output: I should use float2 writes if possible to coalesce better. The output is written by thread 0 for all 64 channels. Thread 0 writes 64 floats sequentially, which is perfectly coalesced (it's a single thread writing contiguous memory). So no need for vectorization there. Let me now write the complete code. I'll also add proper comments for clarity. Wait, I want to double check one more thing: the mask in the reference. ```python mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(-1) ``` `pair_mask` has shape `(B, N, N)`. `unsqueeze(-1)` makes it `(B, N, N, 1)`. Then `a = ... * mask` broadcasts to `(B, N, N, H)`. In my kernel, I use `pair_mask` directly (without unsqueeze) by accessing `pair_mask[b * stride_mask + i * N + k]` and multiplying by that scalar. This correctly applies the mask to all H channels. ✓ Also, `pair_mask` in `get_inputs()` is `torch.ones(...)`. So the mask doesn't actually mask anything for the test, but the kernel must still handle it correctly for general inputs. One subtlety: in the reference, the mask is applied to a and b: ```python a = self.proj_a(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_a(x)) * mask b = self.proj_b(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gate_b(x)) * mask ``` But the unsqueeze makes mask shape (B, N, N, 1), so it masks the third dimension (k) per (i,j). So a[b, i, k, :] is masked by pair_mask[b, i, k], and b[b, j, k, :] is masked by pair_mask[b, j, k]. In my kernel, I'm using the mask correctly. Let me write the final optimized kernel now. Actually, let me also try a small optimization: use `fmaf` for the dot products to help the compiler generate efficient FMA instructions. With the `-O3` and `--use_fast_math` flags, the compiler should already do this, but explicitly writing `acc = fmaf(xv, proj_a_w[c * H + h], acc)` might help. Hmm, `--use_fast_math` includes enabling `use_fast_math` which makes `fmaf` available and encourages the compiler to use FMAs. But let me keep the code clean and rely on the compiler for FMA generation. One final thought: could I load all pair_repr[b, i, :, :] into registers or shared memory at the start of the block to avoid re-reading it for each k? No, because i is fixed, and for each k we read pair_repr[b, i, k, :]. The total is 32 * 64 = 2048 floats = 8KB per block. If we loaded all of it at once, we'd need 8KB of shared memory or registers. Registers: 32 threads * 64 floats = 2048 floats. That's too many registers. Shared memory: 8KB is fine, but then the layer_norm would be done on all k simultaneously, requiring 32 threads to do layer_norm on 2048 values = 64 values per thread. Not impossible, but complicated. The current approach of processing one k at a time is simpler and easier for the compiler to optimize. Actually, given the small size (N=32), we could load pair_repr for fixed (b, i) into shared memory: ``` float pair_i[32][64]; // 2048 floats = 8KB ``` Then for each k, we access `pair_i[k][c]` from shared memory instead of global memory. This avoids re-reading from global memory for the a computation. Similarly, pair_repr for j needs to be read for each k. But the issue is that each block handles one (b, i, j), so loading pair_repr[b, i, :, :] once and pair_repr[b, j, :, :] once means reading 16KB from global memory. Currently we read pair_repr[b, i, k, :] and pair_repr[b, j, k, :] for each k, which is also 16KB total. So no bandwidth savings. Unless... we can share pair_repr[b, i, :, :] across blocks with the same i? But blocks with the same i may be on different SMs. OK, I think the transposed weights optimization is the best bang for the buck. Let me write the code. Actually, let me also think about one more thing. The reference `pair_repr` input might not be contiguous in memory. In my kernel, I call `.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32)` at the start of the C++ function. This ensures contiguity but might cause a memory copy. The reference implementation works directly on whatever layout PyTorch gives. In `ModelNew.forward()`, I should probably also make the inputs contiguous on the Python side before passing to CUDA, to avoid extra copies. Or, I can pass them as-is and use `repr` and `mask` in the C++ function. Actually, the C++ function already does `auto repr = pair_repr.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);` which handles it. But wait, this means that every forward call we potentially allocate new tensors and copy data. The reference implementation might not need to do this if the inputs are already contiguous float32. To be fair, the reference implementation also uses `.float()` implicitly since the module runs in float32 mode (assuming inputs are float32). Actually, looking at `get_inputs()`, `pair_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)`. This creates a contiguous float32 tensor by default. `pair_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len)` also creates a contiguous float32 tensor. So the `.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32)` in the C++ function is a no-op copy and very fast (it might just return the same tensor since it's already contiguous and float32). Wait, `.to(torch::kFloat32)` on a float32 tensor also returns the same tensor. And `.contiguous()` on a contiguous tensor returns the same tensor. So there should be no copy. However, the `.contiguous().float()` on the Python side for the weights might cause copies if the weights are not already contiguous float32. PyTorch nn.Linear weights are typically contiguous float32 by default. So this should also be a no-op. But to be safe, I should avoid unnecessary `.contiguous().float()` calls if they're causing actual copies. For the parameters, since they don't change during inference, I could pre-convert them in `__init__` or the first forward call. But the simplest is to just pass the weights directly and let the kernel handle them. Actually, the weights from nn.Linear are contiguous by default. And they are float32. So the `.contiguous().float()` is indeed a no-op. Wait, there might be one issue: `self.proj_a.weight` is actually float32 by default in the reference? The reference uses standard PyTorch which defaults to float32. Yes. So the copies should be negligible. Let me finalize the code. One more optimization: in layer_norm, the warp reduction uses `warp_reduce_sum`. On modern GPUs (sm_70+), `__shfl_xor_sync` is a single instruction with very low latency. Good. I'll write the complete kernel now.

H-1 (H == 32)\n if (b >= B) return;\n\n const int stride_repr = N * N * C;\n const int stride_mask = N * N;\n const int base_ij = b * stride_repr + i * N * C + j * C;\n\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_x_i = smem; // [C]\n float* smem_x_j = smem + C; // [C]\n float* smem_gate = smem + 2 * C; // [C]\n\n float acc = 0.0f;\n\n for (int k = 0; k < N; ++k) {\n int base_i = b * smem_x_i[h];\n float v1 = smem_x_i[h + 32];\n float sum = v0 + v1;\n sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum);\n float mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C);\n float d0 = v0 - mean;\n float d1 = v1 - mean;\n float sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1;\n sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq);\n float var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C);\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + EPS);\n smem_x_i[h] = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + ln_in_b[h];\n smem_x_i[h + 32] = d1 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h + 32] + ln_in_b[h + 32];\n\n // layer_norm_in on x_j\n v0 = smem_x_j[h];\n v1 = smem_x_j[h + 32];\n sum = v0 + v1;\n sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum);\n mean = sum * (1.0f / (float)C);\n d0 = v0 - mean;\n d1 = v1 - mean;\n sq = d0 * d0 + d1 * d1;\n sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq);\n var = sq * (1.0f / (float)C);\n inv_std = rsqrtf(var + EPS);\n smem_x_j[h] = d0 * inv_std * ln_in_w[h] + mean_out = sum_out * (1.0f / (float)H);\n float diff_out = acc - mean_out;\n float sq_out = diff_out * diff_out;\n sq_out = warp_reduce_sum(sq_out);\n float var_out = sq_out * (1.0f / (float)H);\n acc h + 32];\n\n // out_gate linear (transposed weight => contiguous warp access)\n float gate0 = 0.0f;\n float gate1 = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) {\n float xv = smem_gate[c];\n gate0 += xv * out_gate_w[c * C + h];\n gate1 += xv * out_gate_w[c * C + h + 32];\n }\n smem_gate[h] = sigmoidf_fast(gate0 + out_gate_b[h]);\n smem_gate[h + 32] = sigmoidf_fast(gate1 + out_gate_b[h + 32]);\n\n // Store acc into shared memory for the output projection\n smem_x_i[h] = acc; // reuse smem_x_i as accumulator buffer\n\n // out_proj: each warp iterates over output channels c\n for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) {\n float prod = smem_x_i[h] * out_proj_w[c * H + h];\n prod = warp_reduce_sum(prod);\n if (h == 0) {\n float out_val = prod + out_proj_b[c];\n out_val *= smem_gate[c];\n output[base_ij + c] = out_val;\n }\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor forward_cuda(\n torch::Tensor pair_repr,\n torch::Tensor pair_mask,\n torch::Tensor ln_in_w,\n torch::Tensor ln_in_b,\n pair_repr.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);\n auto mask = pair_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);\n\n int B = repr.size(0);\n auto output = torch::empty_like(repr);\n\n dim3 grid(N, N, B);\n dim3 block(H);\n size_t smem_size = 3 * C * sizeof(float);\n\n fused_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n repr.data_ptr<float>(),\n mask.data_ptr<float>(),\n reasoning
Now I have a working, faster implementation that's numerically accurate. Let me make a few optimizations to improve performance and then submit. Specifically, looking at the code: 1. All weights passed as float32 - this is good 2. But every forward call does `.t().contiguous()` - this creates overheads. Let's pre-transpose and cache them in `__init__`. 3. The kernel is already fused quite well. Let me see if there are memory access improvements. Actually, a key improvement could be to pre-transpose the weights in `__init__` and register them as buffers, so we don't have to call `.t().contiguous()` on every forward pass. This is a clear performance win since those operations require CPU synchronization and memory copies. Let me also check if there are any more optimizations possible in the CUDA kernel. The current kernel has quite a bit of iteration over k (32 iterations per thread block), which seems fine given the small problem size. Let me submit a refined version with pre-transposed weights.

turn 6 · 1 tool call(s) · 52.3s · 21,884 chars (click to expand)
turn 6llm latency: 52.3s
reasoning
The model compiles. Now let me check correctness. I correctly set up the cached parameters, but I need to make sure the `_ln_in_w` and other parameters are indeed `float` and contiguous. Also, I used `cache(self.proj_a.weight)` which calls `w.detach().clone().t().contiguous().float()`. Since `w` might be `float32` already, `detach().clone()` returns a tensor. Then `t()` transposes it. `contiguous()` makes it row-major. `float()` makes sure it's float32. That should be fine. For `out_proj`, I store it as `self.out_proj.weight.detach().clone().float().contiguous()`.
